reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    One full sweep of Gibbs sampling for a multivariate Gaussian with
    known precision matrix.  Each coordinate is sampled from its full
    conditional distribution while holding all other coordinates fixed.
    Benchmarks the sequential-scan pattern common in Bayesian models
    with tractable conditionals (e.g., topic models, Boltzmann machines).
    """

    def __init__(self, dim):
        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim
        # Precision matrix: diagonally dominant for well-conditioning
        L = torch.randn(dim, dim) * 0.1
        precision = L @ L.t() + torch.eye(dim) * 2.0
        self.register_buffer("precision", precision)
        self.register_buffer("mu", torch.zeros(dim))

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            x: (B, dim) – current state
        Returns:
            x_new: (B, dim) – state after one full Gibbs sweep
        """
        x = x.clone()
        for i in range(self.dim):
            prec_ii = self.precision[i, i]
            residual = x @ self.precision[i] - x[:, i] * prec_ii
            cond_mean = self.mu[i] - residual / prec_ii
            cond_var = 1.0 / prec_ii
            x[:, i] = cond_mean + torch.sqrt(cond_var) * torch.randn(x.shape[0], device=x.device)
        return x


dim = 32
batch_size = 4096


def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, dim)]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [dim]

So we can just use the output pointer as our working buffer. But we need to initialize it with the input x. So in the kernel or in Python, we can do `out = x.clone()`. That's one copy. But we can also have the kernel load x[b, j] into registers first and then write to output at the end. But to mimic in-place updates (Gauss-Seidel), we need the updated values for later i. If we keep everything in registers, it's easy: load x into registers initially, update registers, write back at end. That avoids copying x to output first. But we need to read x from input. So: Option A: In Python, `out = x.clone()`, then kernel modifies `out` in place. The kernel reads from `out` each iteration. Since `out` initially is a copy of x, and we update `out[b,i]` each iteration, subsequent reads see the updated value. This is correct and simple. Option B: Kernel loads x into local array, processes, writes to output. This avoids the clone but requires local memory. However, the clone is also a kernel launch (or a memcpy). The cost of clone is negligible (0.5MB). Let's do Option A: `out = x.clone()`, then call kernel that modifies `out` in place. But wait, if the kernel modifies `out` in place and reads from `out`, there might be a race condition: threads in the same warp (or block) reading and writing adjacent memory? No, each thread only reads and writes the memory for its own batch index `b`. There is no sharing across batch elements. However, threads in the same warp will read `out` at different `b` but same `j`. Since `out` is stored in row-major order, `out[b, j]` and `out[b+1, j]` are adjacent. When all threads in a warp read `out[.., j]`, the accesses are coalesced. When they write `out[b, i]`, it's also coalesced. There is no cross-thread dependency. So it's safe. Wait, is there any issue with the compiler caching reads? Since we read `out[b, j]` and write `out[b, i]` in the same loop, but `j` might equal `i` in the dot product? Actually, in the residual calculation: `residual = dot - x_i * prec_ii`. The dot product includes `x_i * prec_ii`. So we compute dot = sum_j x_j * prec_ij. Then subtract x_i * prec_ii. If we are reading x_i from `out[b, i]`, we must be careful that we haven't overwritten it yet in the current iteration. The Gauss-Seidel update uses the old value of x_i for the residual? Wait, in the reference: ```python residual = x @ self.precision[i] - x[:, i] * prec_ii ``` At this point, `x[:, i]` has already been updated in previous iterations? Actually, for the current i, `x[:, i]` is the value from the previous sweep (or from earlier in the same sweep? No, we are at iteration i in the loop. The code first computes `residual`, then computes `cond_mean`, then updates `x[:, i]`. At the time of computing `residual`, `x[:, i]` is whatever value it currently holds. Since we haven't updated it for this i yet in this sweep, it's the value from the previous sweep (or initialization). But note that for j < i, x_j has been updated in this sweep. So the dot product includes the updated x_j. For x_i itself, it's the old value. The formula `x @ precision[i] - x[:, i] * prec_ii` subtracts the contribution of x_i from the dot product, because the full conditional excludes x_i. Now, if we implement the kernel to update x_i at the end of iteration i, then when we compute the dot product for iteration i, we must use the old x_i. If we read x_i from the output array at the same time as other x_j, it's the old value (since we haven't updated it yet). So that's correct. Then we compute cond_mean, cond_var, sample, and then write the new sample to out[b, i]. Next iteration i+1 reads out[b, j] for all j, including j=i which now has the updated value. This matches Gauss-Seidel. So no issue. Therefore, the kernel can simply read from the output buffer. Implementation details: ```cuda __global__ void gibbs_sweep_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ precision, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ noise, int B, int dim) { // load precision into shared memory extern __shared__ float s[]; // We need shared mem for precision (dim*dim) + mu (dim) // We'll dynamically size it. float* sh_prec = s; float* sh_mu = s + dim * dim; int tid = threadIdx.x; int total_prec = dim * dim; // cooperative load for (int idx = tid; idx < total_prec; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_prec[idx] = precision[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < dim; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_mu[idx] = mu[idx]; } __syncthreads(); int b = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (b >= B) return; for (int i = 0; i < dim; ++i) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < dim; ++j) { float xj = out[b * dim + j]; dot += xj * sh_prec[i * dim + j]; } float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * dim + i]; float residual = dot - out[b * dim + i] * prec_ii; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii; float sample = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + b]; out[b * dim + i] = sample; } } ``` Wait, we need to be careful about `#pragma unroll` with runtime dim. The compiler might not unroll if dim is not constant. We can pass dim as a template parameter to allow unrolling. Since we know dim is 32, we can instantiate the kernel with template<dim=32>. But we don't know at module load time? We can pass a constexpr int or use a switch. A common trick is to have a switch for small dims. Since i is the same for all threads (loop over i), and j is the same for all threads in the inner loop, all threads read the same shared memory address simultaneously. Shared memory can broadcast, so it's very fast. The xj reads are coalesced global memory reads. In each i, we do j loop of 32 iterations. Total FMAs per thread = 1024. That's not a lot. Memory reads: 32*32 = 1024 global reads of x (16KB). Noise reads: 32. Writes: 32. Precision reads: 1024 from shared mem. So the kernel is memory bandwidth bound but with very low total work. The main bottleneck in the reference is the overhead of 200+ kernel launches. Our kernel reduces to 1 launch plus 1 noise generation launch (or 2 if we clone). So it should be much faster. Wait, can we also fuse the noise generation into the kernel by using the same `torch.randn` call pattern but inside CUDA? The noise generation requires RNG. We can generate noise in the kernel by calling PyTorch's Philox RNG from C++ extension. Is there a way to do that? So total launches: 2 (clone + noise) + 1 (gibbs). Actually, we can avoid clone by initializing output with a copy kernel or by having the kernel load from input and write to output. Let's avoid clone and allocate output uninitialized, then kernel reads from input and writes to output. But the update must be in-place Gauss-Seidel, so we need to use the updated values as we go. If we read from input (which is unchanged), we wouldn't get the updated values for j < i. So we can't just read from input. We need to read from a mutable array. That's a broadcast, no bank conflicts. This is very clean and avoids cloning and global memory reads during the sweep (except noise). So total global memory reads: DIM (input) + DIM (noise) = 64 reads per thread. Total writes: DIM (output) = 32 writes. That's tiny. Wait, what about precision and mu? Now, we need to write the C++ wrapper and Python code. Let's detail the C++ code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> template <int DIM> __global__ void gibbs_sweep_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ precision, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ noise, float* __restrict__ out, int B) { // Shared memory for precision and mu extern __shared__ float s[]; float* sh_prec = s; float* sh_mu = s + DIM * DIM; int tid = threadIdx.x; int b = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + tid; // Load precision and mu into shared memory for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM * DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_prec[idx] = precision[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_mu[idx] = mu[idx]; } __syncthreads(); if (b >= B) return; // Load x into local registers float x_reg[DIM]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < DIM; ++j) { x_reg[j] = x[b * DIM + j]; } // Gibbs sweep #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < DIM; ++i) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < DIM; ++j) { dot += x_reg[j] * sh_prec[i * DIM + j]; } float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * DIM + i]; float residual = dot - x_reg[i] * prec_ii; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii; float sample = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + b]; x_reg[i] = sample; } // Write back #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < DIM; ++j) { out[b * DIM + j] = x_reg[j]; } } ``` Then a dispatch function: ```cpp torch::Tensor gibbs_sweep_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor precision, torch::Tensor mu, torch::Tensor noise) { int B = x.size(0); int dim = x.size(1); auto out = torch::empty_like(x); const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (B + threads - 1) / threads; size_t shared_mem = dim * dim * sizeof(float) + dim * sizeof(float); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(x.scalar_type(), "gibbs_sweep", [&] { // Only float is expected const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); } else if (dim == 64) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<64><<<blocks, threads, shared_mem>>>( x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else { // generic fallback - can't easily do dynamic local array // But we can launch a dynamic version. // Let's write a dynamic version. } }); return out; } ``` But we need a generic fallback. We can write a non-templated kernel that uses a dynamically sized local array? Actually, for the generic case, we can have a separate kernel that reads x from global memory each iteration. Since we don't expect generic dims in the benchmark, but we need correctness for any dim. Let's write a generic kernel that doesn't use a local array: ```cuda __global__ void gibbs_sweep_kernel_dynamic( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ precision, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ noise, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int dim) { extern __shared__ float s[]; float* sh_prec = s; float* sh_mu = s + dim * dim; int tid = threadIdx.x; int b = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + tid; for (int idx = tid; idx < dim * dim; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_prec[idx] = precision[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < dim; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_mu[idx] = mu[idx]; } __syncthreads(); if (b >= B) return; // We need a mutable copy. Let's allocate a small local array via scratch memory? // Actually, we can write to out and read from out. // Initialize out with x. for (int j = 0; j < dim; ++j) { out[b * dim + j] = x[b * dim + j]; } __threadfence_block(); // ensure visible? Actually within same thread, writes are visible. for (int i = 0; i < dim; ++i) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < dim; ++j) { dot += out[b * dim + j] * sh_prec[i * dim + j]; } float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * dim + i]; float residual = dot - out[b * dim + i] * prec_ii; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii; float sample = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + b]; out[b * dim + i] = sample; } } ``` Wait, for the generic fallback, we need to copy x to out first. That's extra writes. For dim=32, it's fine. However, the templated version is more efficient. We can use the templated version for dim=32 (the benchmark) and the dynamic version for others. But there is an issue: in the dynamic version, we need to ensure that when we read `out[b * dim + j]` in the dot product, we get the latest values. Let's refine the C++ code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> template <int DIM> __global__ void gibbs_sweep_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ precision, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ noise, float* __restrict__ out, int B) { extern __shared__ float s[]; float* sh_prec = s; float* sh_mu = s + DIM * DIM; int tid = threadIdx.x; int b = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + tid; for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM * DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_prec[idx] = precision[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_mu[idx] = mu[idx]; } __syncthreads(); if (b >= B) return; float x_reg[DIM]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < DIM; ++j) { x_reg[j] = x[b * DIM + j]; } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < DIM; ++i) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < DIM; ++j) { dot += x_reg[j] * sh_prec[i * DIM + j]; } float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * DIM + i]; float residual = dot - x_reg[i] * prec_ii; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii; float sample = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + b]; x_reg[i] = sample; } #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < DIM; ++j) { out[b * DIM + j] = x_reg[j]; } } __global__ void gibbs_sweep_kernel_generic( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ precision, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ noise, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int dim) { extern __shared__ float s[]; float* sh_prec = s; float* sh_mu = s + dim * dim; int tid = threadIdx.x; int b = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + tid; for (int idx = tid; idx < dim * dim; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_prec[idx] = precision[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < dim; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_mu[idx] = mu[idx]; } __syncthreads(); if (b >= B) return; // copy x to out, then modify out in place for (int j = 0; j < dim; ++j) { out[b * dim + j] = x[b * dim + j]; } for (int i = 0; i < dim; ++i) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < dim; ++j) { dot += out[b * dim + j] * sh_prec[i * dim + j]; } float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * dim + i]; float residual = dot - out[b * dim + i] * prec_ii; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii; float sample = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + b]; out[b * dim + i] = sample; } } torch::Tensor gibbs_sweep_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor precision, torch::Tensor mu, torch::Tensor noise) { int B = x.size(0); int dim = x.size(1); auto out = torch::empty_like(x); const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (B + threads - 1) / threads; float* out_ptr = out.data_ptr<float>(); const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); const float* prec_ptr = precision.data_ptr<float>(); const float* mu_ptr = mu.data_ptr<float>(); const float* noise_ptr = noise.data_ptr<float>(); // dispatch based on dim for templated kernels if (dim == 4) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<4><<<blocks, threads, (4*4+4)*sizeof(float)>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else if (dim == 8) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<8><<<blocks, threads, (8*8+8)*sizeof(float)>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else if (dim == 16) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<16><<<blocks, threads, (16*16+16)*sizeof(float)>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else if (dim == 32) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<32><<<blocks, threads, (32*32+32)*sizeof(float)>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else if (dim == 64) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<64><<<blocks, threads, (64*64+64)*sizeof(float)>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else { size_t shared_mem = dim * dim * sizeof(float) + dim * sizeof(float); gibbs_sweep_kernel_generic<<<blocks, threads, shared_mem>>>( x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B, dim); } return out; } ``` Wait, we need to ensure `x`, `precision`, `mu`, `noise` are contiguous and on CUDA. In Python, we can enforce `.contiguous()`. Also, `precision` is a 2D buffer of shape (dim, dim). In the reference, it's stored as a buffer. In our kernel, we need a pointer to its data. We can pass `precision.data_ptr<float>()` after ensuring contiguous and float32. The noise generation in Python: ```python def forward(self, x): B = x.shape[0] # Generate noise in the same order as the reference: torch.randn(B) called dim times # This is equivalent to torch.randn(dim, B) in terms of RNG stream noise = torch.randn(self.dim, B, device=x.device) # Call fused kernel return gibbs_sweep_cuda(x.contiguous(), self.precision.contiguous(), self.mu.contiguous(), noise.contiguous()) ``` Wait, we must ensure that `noise` is generated with the same stream order. As discussed, `torch.randn(self.dim, B)` generates the same values as `torch.randn(B)` dim times. Let's double-check. Actually, I want to be absolutely sure. Let's test with a quick snippet mentally: ```python torch.manual_seed(0) a = torch.randn(3) b = torch.randn(3) torch.manual_seed(0) c = torch.randn(2,3) print(torch.allclose(torch.stack([a,b]), c)) ``` Will this be True? I believe yes for CPU. For CUDA, I also believe yes because PyTorch's Philox RNG is a simple sequence. However, I recall that for CUDA, `torch.randn` uses a random kernel that generates numbers in a specific order based on thread index. The state is advanced by the total number of elements. For a tensor of size N, the kernel maps global thread id `tid` to element `tid`. For a 2D tensor of shape (2,3), `tid` maps to `element = tid`. The flattening order is row-major: element 0 = (0,0), 1=(0,1), 2=(0,2), 3=(1,0), 4=(1,1), 5=(1,2). For two separate calls of size 3, the first call maps element 0,1,2 to the first 3 values; second call maps 0,1,2 to the next 3 values. So `c[0]` (shape 3) equals first 3 values, `c[1]` equals next 3 values. But `c` as a (2,3) tensor has elements [v0,v1,v2,v3,v4,v5] where v0..v2 are first call and v3..v5 are second call. So `c[0,:] = [v0,v1,v2]`, `c[1,:] = [v3,v4,v5]`. This matches `a` and `b`. So yes. But what about transposition? In our case, `noise = torch.randn(self.dim, B)` gives shape (dim, B). We need `noise[i, b]` to correspond to the i-th call's b-th element. In the separate calls, the i-th call generates a vector of length B. In the single call, row i corresponds to the i-th chunk of B elements. So `noise[i, b]` is the correct element. Thus, we can pass `noise` and read `noise[i*B + b]` (since it's row-major). Good. One subtlety: the reference draws noise inside the loop, after computing cond_mean and cond_var. Our kernel draws noise before. But the random numbers are the same, just used later. Since the RNG state is advanced the same amount, it's okay. Now, we should check how the precision matrix and mu are passed. In the reference, `self.precision` is a buffer of shape (dim, dim). In our kernel, we assume it's row-major. `precision[i, j]` should be `sh_prec[i*dim + j]`. That matches. Another subtlety: the reference uses `x @ self.precision[i]`. Here `self.precision[i]` is the i-th row. Wait, `x` is (B, dim), `self.precision[i]` is (dim,). In PyTorch, `x @ self.precision[i]` does a matrix-vector multiplication where each row of x is dotted with `precision[i]`. So it's `sum_j x[b, j] * precision[i, j]`. So `precision[i, j]` is the element at row i, column j. In our kernel, we used `sh_prec[i*dim + j]`. That's row i, column j. Good. The residual: `x @ precision[i] - x[:, i] * prec_ii`. So subtract `x[b, i] * precision[i, i]`. In our kernel: `dot - x_reg[i] * prec_ii`. Good. cond_mean = mu[i] - residual / prec_ii. Good. cond_var = 1.0 / prec_ii. Good. sample = cond_mean + sqrt(cond_var) * randn. Good. Then update x[:, i] = sample. Good. So the math is correct. Now, we need to write the Python code with `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. Let's draft the full Python file: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> template <int DIM> __global__ void gibbs_sweep_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ precision, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ noise, float* __restrict__ out, int B) { extern __shared__ float s[]; float* sh_prec = s; float* sh_mu = s + DIM * DIM; int tid = threadIdx.x; int b = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + tid; for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM * DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_prec[idx] = precision[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_mu[idx] = mu[idx]; } __syncthreads(); if (b >= B) return; float x_reg[DIM]; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < DIM; ++j) { x_reg[j] = x[b * DIM + j]; } #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < DIM; ++i) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < DIM; ++j) { dot += x_reg[j] * sh_prec[i * DIM + j]; } float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * DIM + i]; float residual = dot - x_reg[i] * prec_ii; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii; float sample = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + b]; x_reg[i] = sample; } #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < DIM; ++j) { out[b * DIM + j] = x_reg[j]; } } __global__ void gibbs_sweep_kernel_generic( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ precision, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ noise, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int dim) { extern __shared__ float s[]; float* sh_prec = s; float* sh_mu = s + dim * dim; int tid = threadIdx.x; int b = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + tid; for (int idx = tid; idx < dim * dim; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_prec[idx] = precision[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < dim; ++idx) { sh_mu[idx] = mu[idx]; } __syncthreads(); if (b >= B) return; for (int j = 0; j < dim; ++j) { out[b * dim + j] = x[b * dim + j]; } for (int i = 0; i < dim; ++i) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < dim; ++j) { dot += out[b * dim + j] * sh_prec[i * dim + j]; } float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * dim + i]; float residual = dot - out[b * dim + i] * prec_ii; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii; float sample = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + b]; out[b * dim + i] = sample; } } torch::Tensor gibbs_sweep_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor precision, torch::Tensor mu, torch::Tensor noise) { int B = x.size(0); int dim = x.size(1); auto out = torch::empty_like(x); const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (B + threads - 1) / threads; const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); const float* prec_ptr = precision.data_ptr<float>(); const float* mu_ptr = mu.data_ptr<float>(); const float* noise_ptr = noise.data_ptr<float>(); float* out_ptr = out.data_ptr<float>(); if (dim == 4) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<4><<<blocks, threads, (4*4+4)*sizeof(float)>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else if (dim == 8) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<8><<<blocks, threads, (8*8+8)*sizeof(float)>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else if (dim == 16) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<16><<<blocks, threads, (16*16+16)*sizeof(float)>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else if (dim == 32) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<32><<<blocks, threads, (32*32+32)*sizeof(float)>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else if (dim == 64) { gibbs_sweep_kernel<64><<<blocks, threads, (64*64+64)*sizeof(float)>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B); } else { size_t shared_mem = dim * dim * sizeof(float) + dim * sizeof(float); gibbs_sweep_kernel_generic<<<blocks, threads, shared_mem>>>(x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B, dim); } return out; } ''' cpp_source = r''' torch::Tensor gibbs_sweep_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor precision, torch::Tensor mu, torch::Tensor noise); ''' gibbs_ext = load_inline( name='gibbs_ext', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['gibbs_sweep_cuda'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, dim): super().__init__() self.dim = dim L = torch.randn(dim, dim) * 0.1 precision = L @ L.t() + torch.eye(dim) * 2.0 self.register_buffer("precision", precision) self.register_buffer("mu", torch.zeros(dim)) def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor: B = x.shape[0] # Match reference RNG stream: torch.randn(B) called dim times is equivalent to torch.randn(dim, B) noise = torch.randn(self.dim, B, device=x.device) x = x.contiguous() precision = self.precision.contiguous() mu = self.mu.contiguous() return gibbs_ext.gibbs_sweep_cuda(x, precision, mu, noise) ``` Wait, in the `cpp_sources`, we typically put the function declaration. `load_inline` will generate pybind11 wrapper for it. Since the subsequence is the thread index, two different launches with different grid sizes will assign different subsequences to the same linear index. For example, linear index 100 might be handled by thread 100 in a launch with 1 block of 128 threads, but by thread 4 in a launch with 32 blocks of 128 threads (grid size = 4096). So the random number would differ. If this is the case, then `torch.randn(dim, B)` will NOT produce the same values as `torch.randn(B)` called dim times, because the grid size is different. The random numbers are assigned per thread, not per linear index. This is a critical concern. Let's verify by looking at actual PyTorch code. Specifically, if thread `t` processes elements `t, t+stride, t+2*stride, ...`, then the random number for element `t + k*stride` is the `k+1`-th number generated by thread `t`. Let's derive more carefully. Let P = precision matrix. Let D = diag(P). Let L be strictly lower part of P, U be strictly upper part. So P = D + L + U. Equation for i: x_i^{new} = mu_i - (sum_{j < i} P_{ij} x_j^{new} + sum_{j > i} P_{ij} x_j^{old}) / P_{ii} + sigma_i z_i, where sigma_i = 1/sqrt(P_{ii}), z_i ~ N(0,1). From PyTorch source (I recall): In `aten/src/ATen/native/cuda/DistributionTemplates.cu`, there is a function: ```cpp template <typename scalar_t, typename accscalar_t, typename RNG> struct NormalKernel { void operator()(TensorIterator& iter, double mean, double std, RNG gen) { ... at::native::distribution_binary_kernel(...); } }; ``` Actually, the normal kernel often uses `distribution_nullary_kernel` defined in `aten/src/ATen/native/cuda/DistributionTemplates.cu`. Let's approximate: ```cpp template <typename scalar_t, typename accscalar_t, typename RNG, typename dist_t> static void distribution_nullary_kernel(at::TensorIteratorBase& iter, RNG gen, dist_t dist) { ... auto offset_calc = make_offset_calculator<1>(iter); char* out_ptr = (char*)iter.data_ptr(0); auto loop = [=] __device__ (int idx) { auto offsets = offset_calc.get(idx); auto out = reinterpret_cast<scalar_t*>(out_ptr + offsets[0]); auto seeds = at::cuda::philox::unpack(rng_engine_inputs); auto rng = at::cuda::philox::philox_engine(seeds.first, idx, seeds.second); *out = dist(rng); }; at::cuda::launch_loop_kernel(..., loop); } ``` Wait, if `idx` is the linear index (0..N-1), and the Philox subsequence is `idx`, then the random number for each element depends only on `idx` and `base_offset`. This would mean that `torch.randn(N)` and `torch.randn(B)` called multiple times produce the same values for the same linear index offset, regardless of grid size. Because `idx` is the element index, not the thread index. But is `idx` the global linear index of the element in the tensor? In `launch_loop_kernel`, the kernel usually iterates over a range of linear indices. For example: ```cpp __global__ void loop_kernel(int numel, func_t f) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; for (int i = idx; i < numel; i += blockDim.x * gridDim.x) { f(i); } } ``` If `f(i)` uses `i` as the subsequence index, then yes, each element gets a fixed subsequence based on its linear index. I strongly suspect it is True. I can reason: each element's random state is `seed + global_offset + linear_index`. So for `a`, elements 0..3 use offsets `0..3`. For `b`, elements 0..1 use offsets `0..1` (base 0). For `c`, base offset is advanced by 2, so elements use offsets `2..3`. So `c` elements correspond to `a[2:4]`. Thus `torch.cat([b,c])` equals `a`. This is a standard property of counter-based RNGs. The only caveat is if the distribution algorithm consumes more than one random number per element. For normal distribution, does each element consume exactly one uniform or one random number from the base generator? But PyTorch handles this by ensuring that each element gets a separate subsequence (i.e., a large enough gap) so that even if a thread consumes up to, say, 16 random numbers, it doesn't overlap with the next element's subsequence. In the single call of size 4, thread 2 handles global element 2. Thread 2 uses subsequence 2, offset 0. Are the random numbers from (subsequence=0, offset=2) the same as (subsequence=2, offset=0)? In a counter-based RNG, the counter is a 128-bit value. The subsequence and offset are two components of the counter. The mapping from (subsequence, offset) to counter might be: counter = (offset_low, offset_high, subsequence_low, subsequence_high) or some other arrangement. Usually, the counter is incremented as a single 128-bit integer. So (subsequence=2, offset=0) corresponds to counter=2, while (subsequence=0, offset=2) corresponds to counter=2 as well, if the counter layout is linear and the "subsequence" is just part of the counter. Wait, if the counter is a 128-bit integer, then `subsequence` and `offset` are just ways to split the 128 bits. If `subsequence` is the lower 64 bits and `offset` is the upper 64 bits (or vice versa), then (subsequence=2, offset=0) != (subsequence=0, offset=2). So the random numbers would differ. However, in PyTorch's implementation, the `Philox4_32_10` engine is initialized with a `seed` and a `counter`. The `counter` is a 128-bit value (4 x uint32_t). The `unpack` function returns a `std::pair<uint64_t, uint64_t>` representing the seed and the starting counter value? Actually, `philox::unpack` returns a `std::pair<uint64_t, uint64_t>` representing the `seed` and `offset`. The `offset` is a 64-bit integer that is the starting counter value for the entire kernel. Then each thread computes its own 4-word counter as: ```cpp uint4 counter; counter.x = static_cast<uint32_t>(subsequence); counter.y = static_cast<uint32_t>(subsequence >> 32); counter.z = static_cast<uint32_t>(offset); counter.w = static_cast<uint32_t>(offset >> 32); ``` Where `subsequence` is the thread's linear index, and `offset` is the base offset. Wait, if `offset` is the base offset passed to the kernel, and each thread uses its own `subsequence` as the upper part of the counter, then different grid sizes lead to different counters for the same global element. Thus, `torch.randn(N)` and a sequence of smaller calls covering the same indices will produce the same numbers. I am fairly confident that PyTorch uses the element's linear index as the subsequence for distribution kernels. Let me verify by recalling the `bernoulli_` or `uniform_` code. In `aten/src/ATen/native/cuda/DistributionTemplates.cu`, there is: ```cpp template <typename scalar_t, typename accscalar_t, typename RNG> struct BernoulliKernel { void operator()(TensorIteratorBase& iter, double p, RNG gen) { at::native::templates::cuda::random_from_to_kernel(...); } }; ``` But for `normal`, the `normal_kernel` calls `distribution_nullary_kernel` which does: ```cpp distribution_nullary_kernel(iter, gen, [=] __device__ (RNG gen) { return normal_distribution<accscalar_t>(mean, std)(gen); }); ``` Wait, the lambda receives `RNG gen`, and the kernel creates one `gen` per thread? I think the kernel is something like: ```cpp auto rng_engine_inputs = gen->philox_cuda_state(iter.numel()); distribution_nullary_kernel(..., rng_engine_inputs, ...); ``` And the kernel lambda is: ```cpp __global__ void kernel(int N, philox_state rng_engine_inputs, ...) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; for (int linear_idx = idx; linear_idx < N; linear_idx += blockDim.x * gridDim.x) { auto seeds = at::cuda::philox::unpack(rng_engine_inputs); auto rng = at::cuda::philox::philox_engine(seeds.first, linear_idx, seeds.second); out[linear_idx] = dist(rng); } } ``` If `linear_idx` is used as the subsequence, then yes, the element's position determines the random bits. This is a common pattern to ensure deterministic results independent of launch bounds. Therefore, I think `torch.randn(dim, B)` WILL produce the same values as 32 calls of `torch.randn(B)` stacked together. The linear index `k = i*B + b` in the big tensor corresponds to the same linear index within the sequence of calls. Wait, but there is a subtlety with non-contiguous tensors or multidimensional tensors. For a 2D tensor, the linear index is computed by the `TensorIterator` or offset calculator based on the strides. For a contiguous tensor, `linear_idx = i * B + b` (row-major). This matches our desired `i * B + b`. So `noise[i, b]` is at linear index `i*B + b`. In the 32 separate calls of `torch.randn(B)`, each call has its own tensor of size B, contiguous. The element `b` has linear index `b`. But the base offset for the i-th call is `i * B`. So the effective global linear index is `i * B + b`. If the kernel uses `base_offset + linear_idx` or `base_offset` as the counter offset and `linear_idx` as the subsequence, the result is the same. Actually, the key is whether `base_offset + linear_idx` is the combined counter. In the separate calls, the base offset is advanced, but the subsequence (linear_idx) resets to `b`. In the big call, the base offset is constant, and the subsequence is `i*B + b`. Since Philox uses a 128-bit counter that is a combination of subsequence and offset, it's not obvious that (subseq=i*B+b, offset=O) is the same as (subseq=b, offset=O+i*B). BUT! Many implementations treat the 128-bit counter as two 64-bit integers: the lower 64 bits are the "subsequence" (element index), and the upper 64 bits are the "offset". Then increasing the offset by `B` is equivalent to increasing the subsequence by `B` (assuming no overflow). If so, (subseq=b, offset=O+i*B) is identical to (subseq=i*B+b, offset=O). This is because the counter is a single 128-bit integer: `counter = offset * 2^64 + subsequence`. So adding `B` to the offset is the same as adding `B` to the subsequence. Thus, the two schemes produce the same counter values. But does PyTorch implement it this way? In `aten/src/ATen/cuda/PhiloxRNGEngine.h`, the `Philox4_32_10` engine is constructed with a `uint64_t seed`, `uint64_t subsequence`, and `uint64_t offset`. The counter is initialized as: ```cpp uint4 counter; counter.x = static_cast<uint32_t>(subsequence); counter.y = static_cast<uint32_t>(subsequence >> 32); counter.z = static_cast<uint32_t>(offset); counter.w = static_cast<uint32_t>(offset >> 32); ``` If we treat this as a 128-bit integer, the value is `offset << 64 | subsequence`. Then increasing `offset` by `k` adds `k * 2^64` to the counter. Increasing `subsequence` by `k` adds `k` to the counter. So they are NOT the same unless `k` is a multiple of `2^64`, which is impossible. So (subseq=b, offset=O+i*B) corresponds to counter `C = (O + i*B) * 2^64 + b`. (subseq=i*B+b, offset=O) corresponds to counter `C = O * 2^64 + (i*B + b)`. These are different counters. So the random numbers are different. If the kernel uses `linear_idx` as the subsequence and a fixed base offset for the entire kernel, then the big tensor and small tensors have different (subseq, offset) pairs for the same conceptual global element. The thread index is the subsequence. This is the per-thread version. But in `distribution_nullary_kernel`, maybe they pass the linear index as the subsequence, and the offset is the base offset. For the big tensor, idx goes from 0 to dim*B-1. For the i-th small call, idx goes from 0 to B-1, and the offset is advanced by B each time. Let me design this kernel: ```cuda template <int DIM> __global__ void gibbs_sweep_kernel_warp( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ precision, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ noise, float* __restrict__ out, int B) { extern __shared__ float s[]; float* sh_prec = s; float* sh_mu = s + DIM * DIM; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warpid = tid / 32; // 0..7 for block size 256 int lane = tid % 32; int sample_idx = blockIdx.x * (blockDim.x / 32) + warpid; // Load precision into shared memory for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM * DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_prec[idx] = precision[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_mu[idx] = mu[idx]; } __syncthreads(); if (sample_idx >= B) return; float x_local = x[sample_idx * DIM + lane]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < DIM; ++i) { float dot_partial = x_local * sh_prec[i * DIM + lane]; // Warp reduce sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, offset); } if (lane == 0) { float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * DIM + i]; float residual = dot_partial - x_local * prec_ii; // but x_local here is from lane 0! // Actually thread 0's x_local is x[sample_idx * DIM + 0], not x[:, i] // We need x[:, i], which is held by thread i float x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, x_local, i); float residual = dot_partial - x_i * prec_ii; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii; float sample = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + sample_idx]; // Broadcast to all lanes float new_x_i = sample; } float new_x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, new_x_i, 0); if (lane == i) { x_local = new_x_i; } } out[sample_idx * DIM + lane] = x_local; } ``` Wait, there are some issues. Thread 0 has `x_local = x[sample_idx * DIM + 0]`, but we need `x[:, i]` for the residual computation. Thread `i` has `x[:, i]`. So thread 0 needs to get `x[:, i]` from thread `i`. Using `__shfl_sync`: ```cpp float x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, x_local, i); ``` This broadcasts `x_local` from thread `i` to all threads in the warp. Correct! Also, `new_x_i` is computed by thread 0, and we need to broadcast it to all threads so thread `i` can update its register. ```cpp float new_x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, new_x_i, 0); ``` But `new_x_i` is only defined inside the `if (lane == 0)` block. We need to declare it outside. Corrected: ```cpp float new_x_i; if (lane == 0) { float x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, x_local, i); float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * DIM + i]; float residual = dot_partial - x_i * prec_ii; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii; new_x_i = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + sample_idx]; } new_x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, new_x_i, 0); if (lane == i) { x_local = new_x_i; } ``` This is great! Each warp handles 1 sample, 32 threads working together. With 256 threads per block = 8 warps, 8 samples per block. For B=4096, 512 blocks. Excellent utilization. For shared memory: DIM * DIM + DIM floats. For dim=32, that's 1024 + 32 = 1056 floats = ~4.2KB per block. With 512 blocks, each uses its own shared memory allocation. H100 shared memory per SM is 228KB, so we can have multiple blocks per SM. The noise access is `noise[i * B + sample_idx]`. With consecutive sample_idx in adjacent warps? No, sample_idx is consecutive within a block but not within a warp. For warp 0: sample_idx = blockIdx.x * 8 + 0, warp 1: sample_idx = blockIdx.x * 8 + 1, etc. Since `noise` layout is (dim, B), different warps access consecutive elements in the B dimension. So consecutive warps in the same block access memory coalesced within the block. Good. Wait, but memory coalescing is a warp-level concept. Within a warp, all threads access `noise[i * B + sample_idx]` where `sample_idx` is the SAME for all threads in the warp. So all threads in a warp access the SAME address. This is a broadcast, which is highly efficient. For loading `x`: `x[sample_idx * DIM + lane]`. Each warp loads 32 consecutive floats (one sample). Coalesced. Good. For storing `out`: `out[sample_idx * DIM + lane]`. Coalesced. Good. For precision access: `sh_prec[i * DIM + lane]`. In shared memory, each thread accesses a different bank (lane 0 to bank 0, lane 1 to bank 1, etc., for a row of 32 floats). 32 threads access 32 different banks, no conflicts. Perfect! This seems like a really good approach. Wait, but we have an issue with the template. For dim=32, DIM=32, blockDim.x should be a multiple of 32. The generic version should handle any dim, but the warp approach requires dim <= 32 or dim being a multiple... actually, the warp approach requires dim <= warp size (32) because we use one lane per dimension element. Actually, for simplicity, let me write the warp-based kernel specifically for dim=32, and fall back to the original generic kernel for other dims. Let me compute shared memory size and throughput. For dim=32, each iteration does: - 1 shared memory read per thread (precision) - 1 register read (x_local) - 1 FMA - Warp shuffle reduce (register-to-register, very fast) - 1 shared memory read (mu), 1 shared memory read (prec_ii), 1 global memory read (noise) - Some arithmetic operations - 1 warp shuffle broadcast Total work per sample: 32 iterations * about 10 ops = ~320 operations. The order of consumption matters! In PyTorch, `torch.randn(dim, B)` fills the tensor in row-major order (dimension 0 varies slowest, dimension 1 varies fastest). So it's equivalent to: ```python noise = torch.empty(dim, B) for i in range(dim): for j in range(B): noise[i, j] = next_randn() ``` The reference does: ```python for i in range(dim): noise_i = torch.randn(B) # uses noise_i ``` So the sequence of random numbers generated is the same! Both consume `dim * B` random numbers, with the same ordering: for i in range(dim), for b in range(B), get one random number. Wait, but in our current code, we have: ```python noise = torch.empty(dim, B) for i in range(dim): noise[i] = torch.randn(B) ``` This is equivalent to `torch.randn(dim, B)` in terms of sequence. Let me verify: `torch.randn(dim, B)` generates `dim * B` numbers in C-contiguous order. Element (0,0), (0,1), ..., (0,B-1), (1,0), (1,1), ..., (1,B-1), ... Our loop: ```python for i in range(dim): noise[i] = torch.randn(B) ``` For i=0, generates B numbers: (0,0), (0,1), ..., (0,B-1). For i=1, generates B numbers: (1,0), (1,1), ..., (1,B-1). ... So the sequence is the same! Therefore, replacing the loop with `torch.randn(dim, B)` produces the SAME sequence of random numbers from the same seed. For counter-based RNG, I believe it's designed so that if you request N elements at once, the sequence is the same as requesting them in chunks, as long as the chunks are processed in order. Let me verify by thinking about how Philox works: Philox has a key (seed) and a counter. For each call, the counter is incremented by the number of elements generated. So: - One call of 32*4096 elements: counter advances by 131072. - 32 calls of 4096 elements each: counter advances by 4096 each time, total 131072. BUT: does PyTorch save/restore the counter state between calls? No, the global RNG state is persistent. After 32 calls of 4096 elements, the counter has advanced by 131072. After 1 call of 131072 elements, the counter has also advanced by 131072. And the sequence produced is: for position k in [0, 131071], the random value at position k is the same whether you generate all at once or in chunks. This is a fundamental property of counter-based RNGs. So `torch.randn(dim, B)` produces the exact same sequence as the loop of `torch.randn(B)` calls! This is fine but not coalesced (it's one address per warp, not per thread). Actually, within a warp, all threads read the same address `noise[i * B + sample_idx]`. This is a broadcast, and it's highly efficient. Let me draft the kernel: ```cpp template <int DIM> __global__ void gibbs_sweep_kernel_warp( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ precision, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ noise, float* __restrict__ out, int B) { extern __shared__ float s[]; float* sh_prec = s; float* sh_mu = s + DIM * DIM; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warps_per_block = blockDim.x / 32; int warpid = tid / 32; int lane = tid % 32; int sample_idx = blockIdx.x * warps_per_block + warpid; // Load precision and mu into shared memory for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM * DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_prec[idx] = precision[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_mu[idx] = mu[idx]; } __syncthreads(); if (sample_idx >= B) return; // Each thread in warp holds one element of the sample float x_local = x[sample_idx * DIM + lane]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < DIM; ++i) { float dot_partial = (lane == i) ? 0.0f : x_local * sh_prec[i * DIM + lane]; // Warp reduce dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 16); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 8); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 4); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 2); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 1); float new_x_i = 0.0f; if (lane == 0) { float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * DIM + i]; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - dot_partial / prec_ii; float cond_std = sqrtf(1.0f / prec_ii); new_x_i = cond_mean + cond_std * noise[i * B + sample_idx]; } new_x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, new_x_i, 0); if (lane == i) { x_local = new_x_i; } } out[sample_idx * DIM + lane] = x_local; } ``` Wait, I see a subtle issue. When `lane == i`, `dot_partial` is 0. That means the warp's sum excludes `x[i] * prec[i, i]`, giving us the residual directly. Good. Thread 0 gets the full residual and divides by `prec_ii`. But there's a numerical equivalence question. The reference computes: ```python residual = x @ precision[i] - x[:, i] * prec_ii cond_mean = mu[i] - residual / prec_ii ``` Our kernel computes: ```cpp residual = sum_{j != i} x[j] * prec[i, j] cond_mean = mu[i] - residual / prec_ii ``` This is mathematically equivalent but might differ slightly in floating point because: - Reference computes full dot product, then subtracts `x[i] * prec[i, i]` - We exclude `x[i] * prec[i, i]` from the sum directly In the reference: `residual = (sum_j x[j] * prec[i, j]) - x[i] * prec[i, i]` In our kernel: `residual = sum_{j != i} x[j] * prec[i, j]` Mathematically they are identical. Computationally, they perform the same operations in the same order! Both compute the sum of all products and then subtract the diagonal term. The only difference is that we save one multiplication by setting one term to 0. But the sum is the same. Wait, actually no. In the reference, PyTorch computes `x @ precision[i]` via a matrix-vector multiplication. Internally, it does `sum_j x[j] * prec[i, j]`. Then it computes the subtraction. So the operations are the same. In our kernel, we have 31 terms in the sum (since one thread contributes 0). The sum of 31 terms should equal the sum of 32 terms minus the excluded term. But floating point addition is not associative, so the order matters. In PyTorch's matvec, the summation order depends on the implementation. In our kernel, the warp shuffle reduction adds terms in a specific binary tree pattern: - Step 1: lane t += lane t+16 - Step 2: lane t += lane t+8 - etc. This is different from PyTorch's reduction order. So we might get slight numerical differences. In the reference code, `x @ precision[i]` is a single row of a matrix multiplication. PyTorch likely uses some efficient reduction, possibly similar towarp-level reductions on GPU. The tolerance is 1e-4, so small differences from summation order should be fine. Our previous scalar kernel passed with max error ~1e-6. The warp-based kernel might have slightly higher error due to different reduction order, but should still be within 1e-4. Let me also think about the scalar kernel's numerical behavior. In the scalar kernel: ```cpp for (int j = 0; j < DIM; ++j) { dot += x_reg[j] * sh_prec[i * DIM + j]; } ``` This is a simple sequential accumulation in one thread. The reduction order is j=0, 1, 2, ..., DIM-1. In PyTorch, the matvec for one row typically uses some parallel reduction across elements, so the order is different from sequential. But both passed within 1e-6. The warp-based reduction order is: ``` After lane computes local product: t0 += t16, t1 += t17, ..., t15 += t31 t0 += t8, t1 += t9, ..., t7 += t15 t0 += t4, t1 += t5, t2 += t6, t3 += t7 t0 += t2, t1 += t3 t0 += t1 ``` This is a binary tree. The final result might differ slightly, but again, tolerance is 1e-4. I think it should be fine. One more thing: for the scalar kernel, I can optimize the generic version to handle dim=32 correctly too. But for performance, I'll dispatch dim=32 to the warp kernel. Actually, let me keep the code simpler. The warp kernel is for dim=32 specifically. For everything else, use the scalar kernel. Let me now write the complete code. Actually, 32 threads each load 1 float. To use float4, we'd need 8 threads each loading 4 floats. But then each thread would hold 4 x values. The warp reduction would need to be done differently. For simplicity, let me stick with scalar loads. The memory bandwidth for 4096 * 32 * 4 bytes = 512KB for x read, 512KB for out write, plus noise 512KB. Total ~1.5MB. H100 memory bandwidth is 3.35TB/s, so memory time is ~0.5 microseconds. The computation is the bottleneck, not memory. With the warp kernel, each warp does 32 dot products of 32 elements each. Per warp: 32 * 32 = 1024 FMAs, plus reductions and arithmetic. At 67 TFLOPS, 1024 FMAs is basically instantaneous. The latency of warp shuffles is more relevant. Actually, the real issue is that each sample requires 32 sequential iterations with data dependency. In the warp kernel, 32 threads work together on one sample. The latency between iterations i and i+1 limits throughput. Between iterations: 1. All threads compute product -> 1 cycle 2. Warp shuffle reduce -> 4-5 cycles 3. Thread 0 computes -> a few cycles 4. Broadcast result -> 1 cycle 5. Thread i updates -> 1 cycle Total: ~10 cycles per iteration. 32 iterations = 320 cycles. At 2GHz, that's 160 nanoseconds per sample. For 4096 samples, with 512 warps running concurrently (since 512 blocks * 8 warps = 4096 warps, but H100 has limited warp slots), the throughput should be good. Actually, the number of concurrent warps per SM on H100: each SM can have up to 64 warps (2048 threads). But with block size 256 (8 warps), each block takes 8 warp slots. If an SM has 64 warp slots, it can host 8 blocks concurrently. With 132 SMs, total concurrent warps = 132 * 64 = 8448. Our kernel needs 4096 warps. So all warps can be resident concurrently! This is excellent. The bottleneck might actually be instruction throughput or shuffle latency. But it should still be significantly faster than the reference. Let me now write the code. Also, I should check whether there's an issue with warp-level primitives on H100. H100 supports `__shfl_down_sync` and `__shfl_sync` natively. One small optimization: instead of computing `lane == i` every iteration, I can rely on the compiler to optimize it. With unrolled loop and constant DIM, the compiler should generate good code. But `i` is the loop variable, so `lane == i` is a runtime comparison in each iteration. However, the inner loop is unrolled for DIM=32, so each unrolled iteration has `lane == constant`. Wait, with `#pragma unroll`, the loop: ```cpp #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 32; ++i) { float dot_partial = (lane == i) ? 0.0f : x_local * sh_prec[i * 32 + lane]; ... } ``` After unrolling, iteration 0 has `lane == 0`, iteration 1 has `lane == 1`, etc. The compiler can keep `lane` in a register and compare with constants. This is efficient. However, there's a subtle issue: thread divergence. In iteration 0, thread 0 takes the true branch (assign 0) and threads 1-31 take the false branch (compute product). Since the branches have different amount of work, there's divergence within the warp. But the "true" path does less work. In practice, modern GPUs handle this with branch masks. Actually, wait. Both branches do the same amount of work if implemented with a select instruction rather than a branch. The compiler should use a conditional move or bitwise select: `dot_partial = (lane == i) ? 0.0f : x_local * prec`. This compiles to a single instruction (like SELP) without actual branch divergence. Let me verify this is the case. In PTX, it would be something like: ```ptx setp.eq.s32 %p1, %lane, %i; mul.f32 %prod, %x_local, %prec; selp.f32 %dot_partial, 0.0, %prod, %p1; ``` No divergence! Just a predicate and select. Great. Actually, there's another way to compute the residual without any predicate. We can compute the full dot product and then subtract the diagonal term: ```cpp float dot_partial = x_local * sh_prec[i * DIM + lane]; dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(...); if (lane == 0) { float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * DIM + i]; float x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, x_local, i); float residual = dot_partial - x_i * prec_ii; ... } ``` This computes the full dot product (all 32 terms) and then subtracts the diagonal. It uses one extra `__shfl_sync` per iteration but no predicate. Which is faster? The predicate version uses no shuffle for x_i but has a select. The full-dot version uses an extra shuffle. On modern GPUs, a `__shfl_sync` is very fast (~1 cycle latency). A select is also very fast. The predicate version avoids the shuffle but requires the select. Both are probably equivalent. But the full-dot version might be more numerically stable if we care about matching the reference exactly, since we sum 32 terms in the same order (just one thread's product is 0 vs the actual product). Wait, the reference sums 32 terms, then subtracts. Our predicate version sums 31 non-zero terms. The sum order is identical in the warp shuffle tree, but the number of terms is different. Actually, the full-dot version's warp shuffle tree sums all 32 products (one of which is multiplied by 0 for thread i? No, thread i computes `x_local * prec` which is non-zero, then we subtract it later). Let me use the full-dot version to be numerically closer to the reference: ```cpp float dot_partial = x_local * sh_prec[i * DIM + lane]; dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 16); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 8); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 4); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 2); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 1); float new_x_i = 0.0f; if (lane == 0) { float prec_ii = sh_prec[i * DIM + i]; float x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, x_local, i); float residual = dot_partial - x_i * prec_ii; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_std = sqrtf(1.0f / prec_ii); new_x_i = cond_mean + cond_std * noise[i * B + sample_idx]; } new_x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, new_x_i, 0); if (lane == i) { x_local = new_x_i; } ``` Wait, for the residual: `dot_partial` is the sum of `x[j] * prec[i, j]` for all j=0..31. Then `x_i * prec_ii` is subtracted. This is exactly the same computation as the reference: `sum_j x[j] * prec[i, j] - x[i] * prec_ii`. The warp shuffle tree reduction for 32 floats has a specific order: ``` Level 1 (offset 16): t0 += t16, t1 += t17, ..., t15 += t31 Level 2 (offset 8): t0 += t8, t1 += t9, ..., t7 += t15 Level 3 (offset 4): t0 += t4, t1 += t5, t2 += t6, t3 += t7 Level 4 (offset 2): t0 += t2, t1 += t3 Level 5 (offset 1): t0 += t1 ``` This means the final sum in t0 is: `((((((t0+t16)+(t8+t24)) + ((t4+t20)+(t12+t28))) + (((t2+t18)+(t10+t26)) + ((t6+t22)+(t14+t30)))) + (((t1+t17)+(t9+t25)) + ((t5+t21)+(t13+t29))) + (((t3+t19)+(t11+t27)) + ((t7+t23)+(t15+t31)))))` This is a specific tree reduction. PyTorch's matvec also uses some tree reduction, possibly different. But we're within 1e-4 tolerance anyway. I'll use the full-dot approach since it more closely matches the reference computation. One more thought: can I avoid the `__shfl_sync` for x_i by having thread i store its x_local to shared memory? In the scalar kernel, each thread had its own x in registers. In the warp kernel, each thread has one element. To get `x[:, i]`, we could: Option A: `__shfl_sync(0xffffffff, x_local, i)` - direct register transfer Option B: All threads write x_local to shared memory, then thread 0 reads `x_shared[i]` Option A is faster because it avoids a shared memory round trip. I'll stick with Option A. Now, can I also avoid the second `__shfl_sync` for new_x_i? Thread 0 could write to shared memory and all threads read it. But `__shfl_sync` from thread 0 is also very fast. I'll keep using `__shfl_sync`. Actually, to get new_x_i to all threads, `__shfl_sync(0xffffffff, new_x_i, 0)` is the most efficient method. Let me also think about whether there's a way to process multiple iterations with instruction-level parallelism (ILP). Since each iteration depends on the previous (x_local is updated), we can't overlap iterations. But we can overlap the memory loads with the computation. One more thing: in C++, if I declare `float new_x_i = 0.0f;` inside the `if (lane == 0)` block, and then try to `__shfl_sync` it outside, the variable is out of scope. I need to declare it outside: ```cpp float new_x_i; if (lane == 0) { ... new_x_i = ...; } new_x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, new_x_i, 0); ``` This works. For non-lane-0 threads, `new_x_i` is uninitialized before the shuffle, but `__shfl_sync` overwrites it with lane 0's value. In CUDA, reading an uninitialized variable is technically undefined behavior, but since the shuffle overwrites it, and the compiler knows this, it should be fine. To be completely safe: ```cpp float new_x_i = 0.0f; if (lane == 0) { ... new_x_i = ...; } new_x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, new_x_i, 0); ``` This initializes it to 0.0f, avoiding any UB. Similarly for `x_i`: ```cpp float x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, x_local, i); ``` This is fine because all threads call `__shfl_sync`, and thread i's `x_local` is broadcast. Here's the final code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> template <int DIM> __global__ void gibbs_sweep_kernel_warp( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ precision, const float* __restrict__ mu, const float* __restrict__ noise, float* __restrict__ out, int B) { extern __shared__ float s[]; float* sh_prec = s; float* sh_mu = s + DIM * DIM; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warps_per_block = blockDim.x / 32; int warpid = tid / 32; int lane = tid % 32; int sample_idx = blockIdx.x * warps_per_block + warpid; for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM * DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_prec[idx] = precision[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < DIM; idx += blockDim.x) { sh_mu[idx] = mu[idx]; } __syncthreads(); if (sample_idx >= B) return; dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 8); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 4); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 2); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 1); float cond_std = sqrtf(1.0f / prec_ii); new_x_i = cond_mean + cond_std * noise[i * B + sample_idx]; } new_x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, new_x_i, 0); if (lane == i) { x_local = new_x_i; } } out[sample_idx * DIM + lane] = x_local; float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii; float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii; float sample = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + b]; out[b * dim + i] = sample; } } torch::Tensor gibbs_sweep_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor precision, torch::Tensor mu, torch::Tensor noise) { int B = x.size(0); Let me think again about whether `torch.randn(dim, B, device=x.device)` is equivalent to the loop. In the CPU path, I kept the loop for noise generation to ensure exact matching. But actually, for the CPU path, the test probably only tests CUDA. Let me still keep the CPU path correct. For CUDA, `torch.randn(dim, B)` should be equivalent to `dim` calls of `torch.randn(B)` in terms of Philox counter advancement. Let me verify by checking PyTorch source. In PyTorch, the Philox RNG increments the counter by the number of elements generated. So: - `torch.randn(N)` increments counter by N - `torch.randn(M, N)` increments counter by M*N - `torch.randn(B)` called `dim` times increments by `dim * B` The sequence of random numbers is: for `torch.randn(M, N)`, elements are generated in row-major order: (0,0), (0,1), ..., (0,N-1), (1,0), ..., (M-1, N-1). For the loop: ```python for i in range(dim): noise[i] = torch.randn(B) ``` This generates: i=0 -> B elements, i=1 -> B elements, etc. Total dim*B elements. The sequence is the same. So `torch.randn(dim, B)` is functionally equivalent. The CPU path should use it too for consistency. Actually, I'll keep the CPU path simple and just use the original loop. In the warp kernel: ```cpp if (lane == 0) { float x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, x_local, i); ``` But `__shfl_sync` inside an `if (lane == 0)` block might have issues because not all threads in the warp participate. The `mask` is `0xffffffff` (all threads), but only lane 0 executes it. For warp shuffle, all threads in the warp must execute the shuffle instruction, OR the inactive threads must be disabled in the mask. If thread 1 doesn't execute `__shfl_sync`, but thread 0 does with mask `0xffffffff`, the behavior is undefined! Actually, in CUDA, for `__shfl_sync`, all threads named in the mask must execute the instruction convergently. If only lane 0 executes it, the result is undefined. No, that would be wrong. We only update thread i's x_local. Wait, in iteration i, only x[i] changes. Other x[j] for j != i remain unchanged. So only thread i should update its register. Other threads keep their old x_local values. Correct. Now, when `lane == i` does `x_local = new_x_i`, what happens for iteration i+1? Thread i now has the updated value, and all other threads have their values from previous updates (or original). This is correct. One more detail: when computing `x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, x_local, i)`, we get the CURRENT value of `x_local` from thread i. By the time we call this, thread i has already processed all previous iterations and updated its `x_local` accordingly. So `x_i` is the correct updated value. Good. Let me now compile and test this. out[b * dim + i] * prec_ii;\n float cond_mean = sh_mu[i] - residual / prec_ii;\n float cond_var = 1.0f / prec_ii;\n float sample = cond_mean + sqrtf(cond_var) * noise[i * B + b];\n out[b * dim + i] = sample;\n }\n}\n\ntorch::Tensor gibbs_sweep_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor precision, torch::Tensor mu, torch::Tensor noise) {\n int B = x.size(0);\n int dim = x.size(1);\n auto out = torch::empty({B, dim}, x.options());\n\n const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>();\n const float* prec_ptr = precision.data_ptr<float>();\n const float* mu_ptr = mu.data_ptr<float>();\n const float* noise_ptr = noise.data_ptr<float>();\n float* out_ptr = out.data_ptr<float>();\n\n if (dim == 32) {\n const int threads = 256;\n const int warps_per_block = threads / 32;\n const int blocks = (B + warps_per_block - 1) / warps_per_block;\n size_t shared_mem = (32 * 32 + 32) * sizeof(float);\n gibbs_sweep_kernel_warp<32><<<blocks, threads, shared_mem>>>(\n x_ptr, prec_ptr, mu_ptr, noise_ptr, out_ptr, B);\n } else {\n const int threads = 256;\n const int blocks = (B + threads - Even though both should produce the same distribution, the actual sampling from `torch.randn(dim, B)` is different from `for i in range(dim): torch.randn(B)` because the random state is consumed in a different order. That explains the CORRECTNESS failure - not a kernel bug, but different noise. To get the same noise as reference, I should match the exact order. The reference samples in a loop per-coordinate (in the CUDA path). Actually, looking at the reference once more: In the reference forward: ```python for i in range(self.dim): ... x[:, i] = cond_mean + torch.sqrt(cond_var) * torch.randn(x.shape[0], device=x.device) ``` So the reference generates noise one row at a time. My kernel at index 21 generates noise the same way (row by row with a for-loop), so it matches. Now I can optimized further. Let me think about additional optimizations: 1. Use `torch.empty(dim, B); for i in range(dim): noise[i] = torch.randn(B)` - confirmed correct. 2. Register pressure: the scalar kernel uses 32 float registers for x_reg plus the shared memory pointers. With aggressive unrolling, the compiler might spill. But with 255 max registers and relatively simple operations, it should fit. 3. Since the operations are very lightweight, shared memory bandwidth is the likely bottleneck. But with `dim=32` and 256 threads per block, we only have 128 batches per block (with 256 threads). So each warp lane processes one batch, and we have very good memory access patterns. 4. Can we prefetch x values for the next iteration? Not really because we must write back. 5. Can we use __ldg for noise and x reads? Yes, __ldg might help on newer architectures since these are read-only. 6. Can we generate noise inside the kernel using a simple RNG? That would remove the need to call `torch.randn` entirely. But we need reproducibility with the reference. The reference uses `torch.randn` which uses Philox. Implementing the same RNG in CUDA might be complex. For simple speedup, keeping the pre-generated noise is fine. Actually, a key insight: the reference generates `torch.randn(...)` sequentially in Python, and the CUDA kernel path generates noise with a for-loop and copies into `noise`. The overhead of launching `torch.randn(dim, B)` in a loop is small compared to the kernel computation, but we can do better by generating all noise at once. But for correctness, the noise generation order must match. Alternatively, we can generate noise on the fly inside the kernel using a deterministic random number generator seeded by a global state or by batch index. This would eliminate the noise tensor read entirely and allow us to match the reference if we replicate the same RNG. However, torch's RNG state is complex. For now, let me stick with the looped noise generation. Actually, wait: what if we just transpose the noise generation? The reference generates one column at a time. We currently fill `noise[i, :]` one row at a time. That's correct. Wait, is there a way to make it faster? What if we generate the noise tensor using `torch.randn(dim * B)` and then reshape? That would use a single RNG call with a single contiguous block, which is much faster than dim separate calls. But would it produce the same random sequence as the reference? The reference generates `torch.randn(B)` `dim` times sequentially. PyTorch's Philox generator produces a sequence of random numbers. Calling `torch.randn(B)` dim times vs `torch.randn(dim * B)` once may produce different sequences because the counter state is advanced differently. So yes, it would likely break correctness. Let me try a different optimization: generate noise with a single `torch.randn(dim, B)` call but transpose it first, so memory access in the kernel is coalesced. Wait, is it currently coalesced? Currently `noise` is `(dim, B)` and the kernel accesses `noise[i * B + b]`. For a fixed i, `b` varies, so threads in a block access `noise[i * B + b]` where `b` is sequential, meaning sequential threads access sequential memory. That's coalesced! And for reading `x[b * DIM + j]`, threads in a warp access `x[b * DIM + j]` where b is sequential, so they read from consecutive batches. If j=lane, then reads are coalesced. But inside the kernel, `b` is the batch index and each thread works independently. So `x[b * DIM + lane]` is already coalesced for each fixed `lane`. Actually, in the scalar kernel, each thread reads `x_reg[j] = x[b * dim + j]`, which is sequential in j (but j is not warp-lane, it's just a loop index). Within a warp, different threads have different `b`, so for the same `j`, they access `x[b0 * dim + j]`, `x[b1 * dim + j]`, ... which are `dim` floats apart. With `dim=32`, that's not fully coalesced but still within a 128-byte cache line for adjacent threads. If `dim=32`, 32 threads in a warp access `x[b*32 + j]` for 32 different b. Since each float is 4 bytes and b is consecutive, the addresses are 128 bytes apart. That's terrible for coalescing. Wait! The scalar kernel has each thread handle one batch. With blockDim=256 and 256 threads, the warp has threads with consecutive `b`. When they all read `x[b * 32 + j]` for a fixed `j`, the addresses are: - thread 0: x[0*32 + j] - thread 1: x[1*32 + j] - thread 2: x[2*32 + j] ... stride = 32*4 = 128 bytes. This is a strided access pattern. It is not coalesced at all. Each warp issues 32 separate 4-byte transactions spread across memory. That's very inefficient. But the warp-based kernel is much better: - One warp handles one batch - Thread lane `j` reads `x[b * 32 + j]` - All 32 threads in the warp read consecutive elements: x[b*32 + 0], x[b*32 + 1], ..., x[b*32 + 31] - That's perfectly coalesced into a single 128-byte transaction! That's why the warp-based kernel should be much faster if we can get it to pass correctness. The correctness failure was due to different noise generation. To fix the correctness with the warp-based kernel, we just need to match the reference noise generation. The reference generates per-coordinate noise. In the warp kernel, for coordinate i, we need `noise[i * B + b]` for batch b. But if we generate noise per-coordinate (loop), the layout is `(dim, B)`. So the access pattern `noise[i * B + b]` is coalesced in the scalar kernel (bad) and in the warp kernel, only lane 0 reads it (one single access per warp, not coalesced, but it's just one float so it's fine). Wait, but the warp-based kernel failed earlier even though I thought it should match. Let me check: at index 17 (when I used the warp-based kernel with single noise generation), the correctness failed. But at index 21 (when I went back to scalar kernel but with looped noise) it passed. The warp-based kernel itself is correct -- the scalar kernel also passes with looped noise. Wait, no: `noise` is `(dim, B)` so `noise[i, :]` is contiguous. `noise[i * B + b]` where b=blockIdx.x * 256 + threadIdx.x. Threads in a warp access `noise[i * B + b]`, `noise[i * B + b+1]`, `noise[i * B + b+2]`, ... which are adjacent floats! Because they all access the same row i, and b is sequential. That IS coalesced! Similarly, for `x[b * dim + j]` with fixed j, threads access `x[b0*dim + j]`, `x[b1*dim+j]`, which are separated by dim floats. That is strided. But `x` is read column-wise with fixed j for each loop iteration. So for j=0, warp threads access x[b0*32], x[b1*32], etc., stride=32. Not coalesced. How to fix this? Transpose x to be `(dim, B)` layout, so `x[j * B + b]` is accessed. Then for a fixed j, threads access adjacent memory. If we transpose x before the kernel and work with it in transposed form inside the kernel, all accesses become coalesced. But the output needs to be in (B, dim) form. We can write output in transposed form and transpose back. Actually, we can operate entirely in transposed form: - Input x is (B, dim) - Pre-transpose to x_T of shape (dim, B) = x.t().contiguous() - In kernel, for batch b and coordinate j, access `x_T[j * B + b]` - Do the Gibbs sweep - Write output as `out_T[j * B + b]` - Transpose back to (B, dim) = out_T.t() But inside the kernel, the update rule for coordinate i is: ``` dot = sum_j x[j] * precision[i, j] ``` If x is transposed, we can still compute this easily: ``` for j in range(dim): dot += x_T[j * B + b] * precision[i, j] ``` This is coalesced for x_T reads! Each iteration j, threads in a warp access `x_T[j * B + b]`, `x_T[j * B + b+1]`, ... which are adjacent. Perfect coalescing. The precision access in shared memory is the same. The noise access for coordinate i: `noise[i * B + b]` is already coalesced (same as before). The output write for coordinate i: `out_T[i * B + b]` is also coalesced. So the main performance win is coalescing x_T reads. The overhead is two transposes of (dim, B) matrices. But `x.transpose(0,1)` doesn't actually move memory, `.contiguous()` does. The cost of `.t().contiguous()` for (4096, 32) is a small kernel. And similarly for the output. These transposes are probably worth it for the coalescing gain. Now, could the bug be due to the for loop `for (int i = 0; i < DIM; ++i)` and `#pragma unroll` causing issues because the loop counter is used in the `__shfl_sync`? No. Wait, I see a subtle bug possibility: in the reference, when `i=0`, `residual` depends on the ORIGINAL `x[:, 0]`. But in the warp kernel: ```cpp float x_i = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, x_local, i); ``` This retrieves the value that `x_local` has for lane `i`. At the start of iteration `i`, `x_local` for lane `i` has already been updated in previous iterations (for `j < i`). So `x_i` is the UPDATED value. This is correct for Gibbs sampling because the residual formula is: ``` dot = sum_j x[j] * precision[i, j] residual = dot - x[i] * precision[i, i] ``` And `dot` includes the updated `x[i]` (because `x[i]` was set in a previous iteration). So we need to subtract `x[i] * precision[i, i]` to get the contribution from all other coordinates. This is exactly what the reference does: ```python residual = x @ self.precision[i] - x[:, i] * prec_ii ``` Where `x @ self.precision[i]` includes `x[:, i] * prec_ii`, so we subtract it. Since `x[:, i]` has been updated from the previous iteration (if i>0) or is the original value (if i=0), this is correct. So the warp kernel is correct algorithmically. Could the `__shfl_down_sync` reduction be wrong? For 32 elements, the warp shuffle down pattern is: - dot_partial starts as `x_local * sh_prec[i*32 + lane]` - shfl_down 16: lane 0 adds lane 16, lane 1 adds lane 17, ..., lane 15 adds lane 31 - shfl_down 8: lane 0 adds lane 8, ..., lane 7 adds lane 15 - shfl_down 4, 2, 1 After all reductions, `dot_partial` in lane 0 contains the sum of all 32 lanes. In other lanes, it contains partial sums but we only use lane 0's value. This is correct. Wait, but `__shfl_down_sync(mask, var, delta)` retrieves `var` from `lane + delta`. But what if `lane + delta >= 32`? The documentation says it returns the value from the source lane, and if source lane is out of range, it returns the calling thread's own value. In our case: - After shfl_down 16, lanes 16-31 get their own value (since 16+16=32 out of range). But lanes 0-15 get lane+16. - After that, lanes 16-31 still have their original value (or half-sums), but we continue shuffling down. If we keep going, lanes 16-31 will compute their own partial sums that are wrong. But lane 0 only adds lane 16, then lane 8, then lane 4, etc. The values being added to lane 0 are correct because they come from lanes that have correct partial sums. Actually, the classic warp shuffle reduction does: ```cpp dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 16); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 8); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 4); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 2); dot_partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, dot_partial, 1); ``` After step 1 (delta=16): - lane 0: dot_partial_0 + dot_partial_16 - lane 1: dot_partial_1 + dot_partial_17 ... - lane 15: dot_partial_15 + dot_partial_31 - lane 16: dot_partial_16 (self) ... - lane 31: dot_partial_31 (self) After step 2 (delta=8): - lane 0: (dp0+dp16) + (dp8+dp24) - lane 1: (dp1+dp17) + (dp9+dp25) ... - lane 7: (dp7+dp23) + (dp15+dp31) - lane 8: (dp8+dp24) [self, since 8+8=16 and lane 16 has dp16, wait no] Actually, lane 8 shfl_down 8 gets from lane 16.
